border: none; padding: 0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt; height: 34.15pt;" valign="top" width="71"><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;"> </span></p></td><td style="width: 81.0pt; border: none; border-bottom: solid black 1.0pt; mso-border-top-alt: solid black .5pt; mso-border-bottom-alt: solid black .5pt; padding: 0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt; height: 34.15pt;" valign="top" width="108"><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><!-- [if gte vml 1]><v:shape   id="_x0000_s1032" type="#_x0000_t32" style='position:absolute;left:0;   text-align:left;margin-left:40.7pt;margin-top:14.45pt;width:0;height:18.3pt;   flip:y;z-index:251660800;mso-position-horizontal-relative:text;   mso-position-vertical-relative:text' o:connectortype="straight">   <v:stroke endarrow="block"/>  </v:shape><![endif]--><!-- [if !vml]--><span style="mso-ignore: vglayout; position: absolute; z-index: 251660800; left: 0px; margin-left: 48px; margin-top: 18px; width: 12px; height: 27px;"><img height="27" src="file:///C:/Users/User/AppData/Local/Temp/msohtmlclip1/01/clip_image004.gif" width="12"/></span><!--[endif]--></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><!-- [if gte vml 1]><v:shape   id="_x0000_s1030" type="#_x0000_t32" style='position:absolute;left:0;   text-align:left;margin-left:28.2pt;margin-top:.6pt;width:.05pt;height:18.3pt;   z-index:251658752' o:connectortype="straight">   <v:stroke endarrow="block"/>  </v:shape><![endif]--><!-- [if !vml]--><span style="mso-ignore: vglayout; position: absolute; z-index: 251658752; left: 0px; margin-left: 31px; margin-top: 0px; width: 12px; height: 27px;"><img height="27" src="file:///C:/Users/User/AppData/Local/Temp/msohtmlclip1/01/clip_image005.gif" width="12"/></span><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;">Step 5<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">          </span>Step-4</span></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;"> </span></p></td></tr><tr style="mso-yfti-irow: 2; mso-yfti-lastrow: yes; height: 27.4pt;"><td style="width: 77.4pt; border: solid black 1.0pt; border-top: none; mso-border-top-alt: solid black .5pt; mso-border-alt: solid black .5pt; padding: 0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt; height: 27.4pt;" valign="top" width="103"><p style="text-align: justify; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;">Remitting Bank</span></p></td><td style="width: 53.1pt; border: none; border-right: solid black 1.0pt; mso-border-left-alt: solid black .5pt; mso-border-right-alt: solid black .5pt; padding: 0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt; height: 27.4pt;" valign="top" width="71"><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;">Step-3</span></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><!-- [if gte vml 1]><v:shape   id="_x0000_s1027" type="#_x0000_t32" style='position:absolute;left:0;   text-align:left;margin-left:7.05pt;margin-top:1.6pt;width:27.8pt;height:0;   z-index:251655680' o:connectortype="straight">   <v:stroke endarrow="block"/>  </v:shape><v:shape id="_x0000_s1028" type="#_x0000_t32" style='position:absolute;   left:0;text-align:left;margin-left:4.05pt;margin-top:14.85pt;width:30.8pt;   height:.05pt;flip:x;z-index:251656704' o:connectortype="straight">   <v:stroke endarrow="block"/>  </v:shape><![endif]--><!-- [if !vml]--></p><table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0"><tbody><tr><td height="0" width="0"> </td><td width="5"> </td><td width="39"> </td><td width="1"> </td></tr><tr><td height="12"> </td><td> </td><td align="left" colspan="2" valign="top"><img height="12" src="file:///C:/Users/User/AppData/Local/Temp/msohtmlclip1/01/clip_image006.gif" width="40"/></td></tr><tr><td height="6"> </td></tr><tr><td height="12"> </td><td align="left" colspan="2" valign="top"><img height="12" src="file:///C:/Users/User/AppData/Local/Temp/msohtmlclip1/01/clip_image007.gif" width="44"/></td></tr></tbody></table><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;"> </span><p> </p><br clear="ALL" style="mso-ignore: vglayout;"/><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;">Step-6</span></p></td><td style="width: 81.0pt; border-top: none; border-left: none; border-bottom: solid black 1.0pt; border-right: solid black 1.0pt; mso-border-top-alt: solid black .5pt; mso-border-left-alt: solid black .5pt; mso-border-alt: solid black .5pt; padding: 0in 5.4pt 0in 5.4pt; height: 27.4pt;" valign="top" width="108"><p align="center" style="text-align: center; tab-stops: 27.0pt; margin: 0in 0in 6.0pt 0in;"><span style="font-size: 9.0pt;">Collecting/Presenting Bank</span></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p style="margin: 6pt 0in 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; color: white; mso-color-alt: windowtext;">Step-1: Seller ships the goods to the buyer's country as per the sales contract. Step-2: Seller presents documents to the remitting Bank with clear instruction for collection of payment. Step-3: Remitting Bank forwards the docume</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">nts and collection instruction to the collecting/presenting Bank. Step-4: Collecting/ presenting Bank presents documents to the buyer for payment. Step-5: Buyer pays for the document. Step-6: Collecting/presenting Bank remits the net proceeds to the remitting Bank <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">after deducting its own charges. Step-7: Remitting</span> Bank credits the net proceeds into the seller's account after deducting its own charges.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">37.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The respondent No. 2 acted in the transaction as the collecting Bank and the presenting Bank, having acted under the collection instructions of the remitting Bank, i.e. Brac Bank Limited (respondent no. 4). Under the aforesaid instructions, respondent no. 4 authorized the respondent no. 2 to handle documents on a collection basis and to release documents to the respondent no. 1 (Milestone Clothing Resources LLC) upon receipt of respondent no. 1's letter of undertaking to pay the collection in 90 days. Respondent no. 2 duly carried out its obligations in such capacity and released the documents to respondent no. 1 after receiving their signed letter of undertaking.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">38.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">Thus, the contractual relationship under URC-522 existed between respondent no. 2 and respondent no. 4 (remitting bank), to which the petitioner-company had no privy. In a “Documentary Collection” under URC-522, it is now established that URC-522 does not create privity of contract between the principal/seller and the collecting Bank. As the collecting Bank is the agent of the remitting Bank, there is no privity of contract between him and the customer, i.e. the seller. This is based on the principle that there is normally no privity of contract between a principal and his sub-agent. Even if the remitting Bank engages a collecting Bank, which is specifically nominated by the customer and who is not one of the remitting Bank's usual correspondences, there is no privity of contract between the collecting Bank and the customer. The High Court of Justice, Queen's Bench Division, Commercial Court, in the case of Grosvenor Casinos Limited v National Bank of Abu Dhabi, reported in 2 All ER (Comm) 112, held that URC-522 does not create privity of contract between the principal and the collecting Bank. To conclude the issue in a simpler version, all that I record here is that there being no ‘offer’ by the petitioner-company to Wells Fargo Bank, resultantly, there is no ‘acceptance’ from Wells Fargo Bank and, thus, there is no consideration and, therefore, it is crystal clear that there is no existence of any contract between the petitioner-company and the respondent no. 2.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">39.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">I would, now, take up the third issue, namely, whether the legal notice sent by the petitioner’s lawyer to the collecting Bank and the reply thereto by the collecting Bank compose an implied form of arbitration agreement under the provisions of Section 9(2)(b)&amp;(c) of the Arbitration Act.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">40.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">It would be useful, if we look at the provisions of Section 9 of the Arbitration Act, which run as follows:</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">9. Form of arbitration agreement-</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">(1) An arbitration agreement may be in the form of an arbitration clause in a contract or in the form of a separate agreement.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">(2) An arbitration agreement shall be in writing and an arbitration agreement shall be deemed to be in writing if it is contained in-</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 1in; text-indent: -22.5pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(a)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">       </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">a document signed by the parties;</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 1in; text-indent: -22.5pt; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(b)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">      </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">an exchange of letters, telex, telegrams Fax, E-mail or other means of telecommunication which provide a record of the agreement; or </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 1in; text-indent: -22.5pt; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">(c)<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">       </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">an exchange of statement of claim and defence in which the existence of the agreement is alleged by one party and not denied by the other. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Explanation-</span></em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The reference in a contract is a document containing an arbitration clause constitutes an arbitration agreement if the contract is in writing and the reference is such as to make that arbitration clause part of the contract. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">41.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">From the marginal note (Head note) of Section 9 of the Arbitration Act, it is vividly clear that the provisions of this Section are about the form of arbitration agreement. Section 9(2) of the Arbitration Act provides, among others, that an arbitration agreement must be in writing and the agreement may be deemed to be in writing if it is contained in a contract signed by the parties or contained in written communication as elaborated in clauses (a) to (c) under Section 9(2) of the Arbitration Act. It further appears that Section 9(2)(b) of the Arbitration Act is not an alternative to Section 9(1) of the Arbitration Act, but an explanation to determine whether an arbitration agreement is in writing or not. As per Section 9(1) of the Arbitration Act, an arbitration agreement can be in the form of a clause in a contract or as a separate agreement. Section 9(2) of the Arbitration Act went further to explain that such agreement as mentioned in Section 9(1) of the Arbitration Act should be in writing, in other words, oral agreement is not acceptable. In addition, Section 9(2) of the Arbitration Act also enlightens that an arbitration agreement will be considered as written if any of the conditions, laid down under it, is satisfied. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">42.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">For determining the existence of an arbitration agreement through Section 9(2)(b) of the Arbitration Act, the following conditions are to be satisfied: (1) the subject matter of agreement i.e. arbitration agreement shall be mentioned in a letter, telex, telegram, email, fax or any other medium and (2) the party on whom such agreement has been served shall agree to such communication. If the second condition as aforesaid is disregarded, and Section 9(2) of the Arbitration Act is found independent, then it would make arbitration obligatory only on the willingness of one party. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">43.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">For determining the existence of an arbitration agreement through Section 9(2)(c), the following conditions are to be satisfied: (1) the subject matter of agreement i.e. arbitration agreement shall be mentioned in an exchange of statement of claim or defence and (2) the agreement is alleged by one party, and not denied by the other. It appears from the second condition that the affirmation to enter into an arbitration agreement is confirmed, by the party to whom such statement of claim of defence has been served, through omission to decline such proposal. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">44.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In other words, under Section 9(2) of the Arbitration Act, an arbitration agreement must be in writing though no special form is prescribed for that. Such an agreement can be in one document or can be gathered from several documents also. It can be gathered from correspondences consisting of letters, fax messages, telegrams or even telex messages. An arbitration agreement can also be constituted when the existence of the agreement is depicted in a statement of claim and defence and the existence of the contract is alleged by one party and not denied by the other. The arbitration clause may exist either in the circumstances by way of incorporating an arbitration agreement or in the situation as contemplated in Section 9 of the Arbitration Act. An arbitration agreement may be included by way of implied terms. For example, the agreement in World Cotton Association by way of implied term may incorporate an arbitration clause. Similarly, in documentary collection, if the parties by express term decide to include ICC Arbitration Rules in their contract, then the parties shall be bound by such term. In the instant case, from the documents on record, it appears that there is no implied term for any arbitration.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">45.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">From the above discussions, it leads me to hold that in order to constitute an arbitration agreement, a written document must be present, which states or refers or demonstrates the <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">ad idem</em> intention of the parties to adopt a quasi judicial method, i.e., a private tribunal as to dispute resolution, whose decision shall be binding upon both the parties.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">46.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-style: italic;">In<em> Thomson Press India Limited v N.C.T. of Delhi, </em>reported in<em> 1999 (1) Arb </em></span><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">LR</span></em><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">421</em>, the Court decided that even though, an arbitration agreement does not necessarily have to include words like 'arbitrator' or 'arbitration', the words used for the purpose must be words of choice and determination to go to arbitration. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">47.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-style: italic;">In Jagdish Chander v Ramesh Chander (2007) 5 SCC 719, the Court decided </span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">that the essential elements of an arbitration agreement are, (i) the agreement should be in writing; (ii) the parties should have agreed to refer any dispute between them to the decision of a private tribunal; (iii) the private tribunal should be empowered to adjudicate upon the disputes in an impartial manner and (iv) the parties should have agreed that the decision of the private tribunal in respect of the disputes shall be binding upon them. Ujwala Raje Shah v Veer Corp. 2014 (2) RAJ 598, connotes "Arbitration Agreement as, "If the terms of the agreement indicate intention on the part of all the parties to refer the dispute to the arbitral tribunal for adjudication and their willingness to be bound by the decision of such tribunal, such agreement would amount to arbitration agreement.” </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">48.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">If I now turn back to the facts of the present case with a view to see the applicability of the above principles of law, it would appear that since Wells Fargo Bank expressly declined their intention to settle the dispute through arbitration, the existence of an arbitration agreement can be found neither under Section 9(2)(b) of the Arbitration Act, due to the lack of their consent to enter into such agreement, nor under Section 9(2)(c) of the Arbitration Act, due to their express denial. In other words, from the communications dated 05.10.2017 and 14.11.2017 between the petitioner and the respondent no. 2, there seems to have no indication or reference to any expression of either party to a pre-meditated consensus to refer any potential dispute to an arbitrator for dispute resolution and hence, while considering whether there exists an arbitration agreement between the parties under reference, the communications dated 14.11.2017 and 05.10.2017 do not attract Section 9(2)(b) &amp; (c) of the Arbitration Act. By merely raising a dispute, an arbitration does not come into play as a matter of course. Thus, I find that the petitioner's legal notice dated 05.10.2017 to the respondent no. 2, and the reply dated 14.11.2017 to the legal notice do not attract Section 9(2)(b) and (c) of Arbitration Act. Section 9(2)(b) and (c) of Arbitration Act would have applied only if the aforesaid legal notice by the petitioner and the reply by the respondent no. 2 contained an agreement, i.e. meeting of the minds, to refer disputes between these parties to arbitration. The reply dated 14.11.2017 issued by the respondent no. 2 does not contain indication whatsoever that the respondent no. 2 was willing to refer the dispute to arbitration. To the contrary, the respondent no. 2 clearly stated that if the petitioner was to pursue any legal action against the respondent no. 2, the only proper forum would be USA Courts. Thus, there was no arbitration agreement between the petitioner-company and Wells Fargo Bank, even through written correspondence. By no stretch of imagination, thus, it can be said that there exists any arbitration agreement between the petitioner-company and Wells Fargo Bank under Sections 9(2) &amp;(3) of the Arbitration Act. However, since Coreattire Fashion (respondent no. 6) through its reply letter dated 04.02.2018 (Annexure H) made the statement <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">“My client proposes to appoint one arbitrator for my aforesaid client himself</em>” (at paragraph 6), this scenario satisfies all the conditions under Section 9(2)(b) of the Arbitration Act, for, the above-quoted statement amounts to a consent to settle the dispute through arbitration. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">49.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-style: italic;">Now, let me deal with the issue no. 4, namely, if the arbitral tribunal is not formed by appointing an arbitrator, whether the petitioner-company is going to be non-suited or remains remediless. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">50.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">There is no scope under the Arbitration Act to refer a dispute to arbitration only on the ground of financial hardship or any other inconvenience of a party, as has been pleaded in this case that since the petitioner is a Bangladeshi party and the counterparty to the dispute is a resident in USA, it would not be feasible to contest the suit in the USA Court given the value of the subject-matter of arbitration is trivial. The basis for appointing an arbitrator is the existence of an arbitration agreement, and not the supposed inconvenience of a particular party. If it is possible to appoint an arbitrator without the existence of an arbitration agreement, any and all parties can apply to the Court for appointment of arbitrators by pleading inconvenience in pursuing the relevant remedy under the applicable law. This would make arbitration agreements, which are foundations of all arbitrations and the law of arbitration, entirely redundant. If this Court takes a view that a party may maintain an application under Section 12 of the Arbitration Act even in the absence of a <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">prima facie</em> arbitration agreement on the ground of inconvenience, the whole scheme of the Arbitration Act would become frustrated and a floodgate would open allowing a party to any civil dispute to apply to Court for constitution of an arbitral tribunal. In my opinion, such a result would be opposed to public policy.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-style: italic;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">51.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; mso-bidi-font-style: italic;">T</span><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">here is no bar to a suit against the importer and the collecting Bank in Bangladesh if a case of fraud or otherwise can be established. The plaint in such event has to disclose a valid cause of action to maintain the suit in Bangladesh. If the Bangladeshi party can establish that the cause of action arose wholly or in part in Bangladesh, a Court in Bangladesh may also exercise jurisdiction over such dispute (Section 20 of the Code of Civil Procedure, 1908). The difficulty lying here may be that there is no reciprocity of enforcement of Judgment between USA and Bangladesh. Therefore, even if a verdict is obtained in Bangladesh, the same cannot be enforced in USA. However, the verdict of Bangladeshi civil Court may be enforced, in a roundabout manner, with the assistance of Bangladesh Bank and other scheduled Banks. For example, if the petitioner-company somehow comes to know that the importer or the Wells Fargo Bank has any money in any Bank in Bangladesh or has stored any goods anywhere in Bangladesh, it may seek to attach the same by the order of the Court. <span style="mso-bidi-font-style: italic;">My considered view, thus, is that since the petitioner-company is free to sue the respondent no. 2 (Wells Fargo Bank) and respondent no. 1 (importer) either in the competent Court of USA or in Bangladeshi civil Court and, further, since the petitioner-company can commence arbitration in Bangladesh any time with the importer (respondent no. 1), the petitioner-company is not going to be either non-suited or remain remediless.</span></span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">52.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The last issue to be dealt with by this Court is whether the collecting Bank is under any obligation to recover money from the drawee (Importer). In case of a transaction through letter of credit ("L/C"), an issuing Bank (the buyer's or importer's Bank) unconditionally guarantees to pay the seller's Bank when the shipping documents, as listed in the L/C, are presented in an acceptable condition. However, in a ‘Documentary Collection’ arrangement (“D/A"), as per Article 16 of the URC-522, the collecting Bank is responsible to make payment only if it has collected payment from the buyer. It has no independent obligation to make payment, if the buyer has not made payment of the agreed amount to the collecting Bank. URC-522 clearly sets out the respective obligations of the parties, and does not contain any provision requiring the collecting Bank to make any payment if it has not received the amount concerned from the buyer. Thus, the difference between an L/C transaction and D/A transaction is that in the former case the buyer's Bank guarantees to the L/C beneficiary Bank (i.e. exporter's Bank) that it would make payment on receipt of compliant shipping documents, whereas in the latter case the buyer's Bank does not provide any such independent guarantee/undertaking. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">53.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In this case, the collecting Bank had engaged in a ‘Documentary Collection’ with an obligation to send the money to the remitting Bank, only when the importer will make payment under D/A. Under the provisions of ‘URC-522', the collecting Bank is under no obligation to take any collateral security towards securing the deferred payment (D/A) at the time of releasing the documents to the drawee, unless the collecting Bank is otherwise instructed by the drawer. URC-522 does not impose any obligation upon the collecting Bank to obtain collaterals from the drawee; it merely obliges the collecting Bank to comply with the collection instructions provided by the drawer. In order to protect its interest, the collecting Bank, sometimes, becomes tempted to release the documents under a trust receipt. Under such a document a genuine trust is constituted, the drawee being the trustee, the collecting Bank being the beneficiary and the proceeds of sale of the goods collected by the drawee being the trust property. In other words, the collecting Bank may enter into any arrangement with the drawee for securing the collecting Bank's interest; so long such arrangements do not contravene the collection instructions by the drawer or the remitting Bank. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">54.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">In this case, the collecting Bank had received instructions from the remitting Bank merely to pass the documents onto the importer upon taking an undertaking from the importer that the latter shall pay within 90 days and, accordingly, the collecting Bank has performed its duties. Whether the collecting Bank has acted in good faith and has exercised reasonable care in dealing with the petitioner-company’s ‘Documentary Collection’, as per the provisions of Article 9 of the URC-522, or it has violated any provisions of Unified Commercial Code (UCC), which has been termed by the learned Advocate for the petitioner-company to be a law of the USA, -is a question of fact to be examined by the Court, in absence of any arbitral tribunal which could not be constituted due to non-incorporation of an arbitration clause in the correspondences of “Documentary Collection”. Now-a-days, many of the remitting Banks inscribe the provisions of arbitration in the L/C and Documentary Collection. It has become a frequent custom to incorporate the provision of arbitration in dealing with Documentary Collection, more particularly in dealing with transactions relating to Documents by Acceptance (D/A). Since the practice of “Documentary Collection” is highly specialized and often counter-intuitive to the general commercial lawyer, the judicial process has not generally afforded speedy, final and sound relief desired by the parties to a dispute under Documentary Collection. To resolve this predicament, International Centre for Letter of Credit Arbitration INC was founded in 1996, which is located in Washington DC, USA. This Centre has established a set of rules, namely, ICLOCA Rules of Arbitration. These rules are modelled to assist in the resolution of disputes arising out of international banking operations, including Letters of Credit, Confirmations or Advices, Documentary Collections, Funds Transfers etc. The interested parties of the transactions may refer the dispute under ICLOCA Rules by inserting a clause into the undertaking or agreement providing that all future disputes, arising out, or in connection to the agreement be subjected to the Centre for Resolution under those Rules. There are two circumstances in which a dispute might be referred to arbitration under ICLOCA Rules and administered by the centre: (i) a clause may be inserted into the undertaking or agreement providing that all future dispute arising out of, in connection with or relating to, that undertaking or agreement be submitted to the centre for resolution under these rules, (ii) an existing dispute may also be referred to the centre for resolution by agreement of the parties, even if there was no advance agreement to arbitration. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">55.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">There are different models of law governing the contractual relationship between the parties. Once the parties adopt any such model in their contractual relationship, the arbitration clause incorporated in such model impliedly becomes applicable in their contractual transactions. For example, if the parties adopt ICC Rules of Arbitration or Rules of the International Cotton Association Limited, the arbitration clauses incorporated therein become impliedly applicable and binding in their contract. Therefore, in “Documentary Collection”, if the parties by express term decide to include ICC Arbitration Rules or ICLOCA Rules of Arbitration in their contract, then the parties shall be bound by such term. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">56.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The parties to a transaction have the flexibility to agree to the dispute resolution mechanism to be applied to a transaction. Such agreement varies from one transaction to another, depending on various commercial and legal considerations. There is no uniform practice of any specific dispute resolution mechanism. The URC-522 does not contain any provision to the effect that the parties to a D/A transaction shall refer their dispute to arbitration.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">57.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Since there is no privity between the principal and the collecting bank, in England, the customer/principal may invoke the Contracts (Rights of Third Parties) Act, 1999 to sue the collecting Bank. Since no such Act is prevalent in Bangladesh, the principal cannot sue the collecting Bank. In such circumstances, the proper remedy of the customer would be to request the remitting Bank, in return for an indemnity against the incurred expenses to claim against the collecting Bank. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">58.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In this case, admittedly the transaction of this case took place under the URC-522 and since the collecting Bank, has no obligation to take any collateral security towards securing the deferred payment at the time of releasing the documents to the drawee (importer) under the URC-522, the collecting Bank is not bound to take any initiative to recover the collections (money) from the importer.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 109%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%; color: black;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">59.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 109%;">After carrying out the examination of all the five issues, as framed by me for the purpose of adjudication of this case, I find that this case (application under Section 12 of the Arbitration Act) is liable to fail against the respondent no. 2 (collecting Bank), mainly, on the grounds of non-existence of the arbitration agreement, albeit the learned Advocate for the petitioner has put his best effort to make out a case on the strength of Preamble, Section 2(c) and Section 9(2)(b) of the Arbitration Act, 2001. He has particularly emphasized on the Preamble of Arbitration Act, 2001 in an expectation to pursue this Court that the scheme and object of repealing the old law of arbitration and enacting the new Arbitration Act in 2001 is to facilitate arbitration for any ‘international commercial dispute’ to be held in Bangladesh. In order to address the above submissions, it would be pertinent to briefly know about the history of arbitration in our jurisdiction. In this part of the world (sub-continent), the concept of arbitration is known to the people from time immemorial. In good old days, disputes between private individuals used to be placed before ‘Panchas’ and ‘Panchayats’. Likewise, commercial matters were decided by ‘Mahajans’ and ‘Chambers’. Formal arbitration proceedings, however, had come into existence after the Britishers started commercial activities in India. The provisions relating to arbitration were found in the Code of Civil <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">Procedure, 1859 (CPC) and, then, in subsequent</span> CPCs. A full-fledged law pertaining to arbitration in India was the Arbitration Act, 1899. A consolidated and amended law relating to arbitration was passed in 1940, known as the Arbitration Act, 1940. <span style="color: black;">Protracted, time-consuming, atrociously expensive and complex Court-procedures impelled the commercial world to an alternative, less formal, more effective and speedy mode of resolution of disputes by a Judge of choice of the parties which culminated into passing of an Arbitration Act. Experience, however, belied expectations. Proceedings became highly technical and thoroughly complicated. The provisions of the Act made 'lawyers laugh and litigants weep'. Representations were made from all quarters of the society to amend the law by making it more responsive to contemporary requirements. Moreover, apart from arbitration, conciliation has been getting momentum and worldwide recognition as an effective instrument of settlement of disputes. There was no composite statute dealing with all matters relating to arbitration and conciliation. The United Nations Commission on International Trade Law (UNCITRAL) adopted a Model Law in 1985 on International Commercial Arbitration. The General Assembly of the United Nations recommended its member-States to give due consideration to the Model Law to have uniformity in arbitration procedure which resulted in passing of the Arbitration Act, 2001. The Act is almost a complete Code in itself and consolidates and amends the law relating to domestic arbitration, international commercial arbitration and enforcement of <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">foreign arbitral awards. The Preamble expressly</span> refers to International Commercial Arbitration.</span></span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">60.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Thus, with the passage of time, the customs and laws relating to arbitration of disputes have achieved the status of the finest law of our jurisdiction, as now it contains both procedural and substantive laws. It has clearly now categorized the provisions for local arbitration and the international arbitration. The latest position of the laws relating to arbitration of all the countries of the world is, which has now become an inviolable law, like Veda’s statement, that there cannot be any arbitration without any arbitration agreement. I find the submissions of the learned Advocate for the petitioner-company to be a desperate attempt to bring his case within the purview of arbitration agreement, although his agreements do not attract the Preamble, Section 2(c) and Section 9(2)(b) in any manner. Since, this Court finds that there is no existence of any arbitration agreement with the Well’s Fargo Bank, this application cannot succeed against it. However, the arbitration may be held with Coreattire Fashion Ltd (respondent no. 6) who had consented to the proposal of arbitration given by the petitioner-company. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">61.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">Before parting with the Judgment, I find it pertinent to make an observation that the Legislature may consider incorporation of a provision at an appropriate place in the Arbitration Act that the Court (the District Judge Court and the High Court Division) shall examine the issue of existence of an arbitration agreement without entering into the merit of the case, when an application for any interim order or for appointment of an arbitrator is made before the Court. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">62.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">I must record here the invaluable assistance rendered by Mr. Ahsanul Karim who appeared before this Court as an Amicus Curie. He took the pain of going through all the relevant laws of Bangladesh and the up-to-date Legal Instruments on International Trade and presented a very precious written submission, significant portions of which have been used by me in this Judgment. Although, currently, Mr. Ahsanul Karim is an Advocate of this Court with a single Star, however, it is hoped that very soon he will be honoured with double Stars by the Honourable Appellate Division, for, in my estimation, he possesses all the qualifications, knowledge, experience and professional etiquette to be endowed with the professional status of a ‘Senior Advocate’. Also, the performances of the learned Advocate for the petitioner Mr. Syed Mohidul Kabir and the learned Advocate for the respondent no. 2 Dr. Sharif Bhuiyan were highly commendable in assisting this Court.</span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">63.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">In the result, this application is rejected. However, there shall not be any order as to costs. </span></p><p style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">64.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The Office is directed to send a copy of this Judgment to the Legislative Division of the Ministry of Law, Justice and Parliamentary Affairs for their consideration as to incorporation of the proposed provision, as observed by this Court in the penultimate para of this Judgment.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">         </span>Ed.</span></strong></p><div style="text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportFootnotes]--><br clear="all"/><hr align="left" size="1" width="33%"/><!--[endif]--><div id="ftn1" style="mso-element: footnote;"><p class="MsoNormal"><a href="file:///H:/article/Issue_May____19(1)/Issue%20_May__19__Arbitration%2005%20of%2018__Muhammad%20Khurshid%20Alam%20Sarkar,%20J__5.doc#_ftnref1" name="_ftn1" style="mso-footnote-id: ftn1;" title=""><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 10.0pt; mso-bidi-font-size: 11.0pt;">Arbitration </span></strong></a><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 10.0pt; mso-bidi-font-size: 11.0pt;">Application No. 05 of 2018</span></strong></p></div></div></div></div>
759	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">Government of Bangladesh Vs. AMS Faraj Construction, (Muhammad Imman Ali, J.) </h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Case No:</b> CIVIL APPEAL No. 181 OF 2016  </p><p><b>Judge:</b>  Syed Mahmud Hossain, CJ Muhammad Imman Ali, J Hasan Foez Siddique, J Mirza Hussain Haider, J.  </p><p><b>Court:</b> Appellate Division ,</p><p><b>Advocate:</b> Mr. Mahbubey Alam, Attorney General, instructed by Mr. Haridas Paul, Advocate-on-Record  ,</p><p><b>Citation:</b>  2019(1) LNJ (AD)  </p><p><b>Case Year:</b>  2018</p><p><b>Appellant:</b>  Government of Bangladesh and others  </p><p><b>Respondent:</b>  M/S. AMS Faraj Construction and another  </p><p><b>Subject:</b>  Constitution of Bangladesh</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  2019-12-04</p><!DOCTYPE html><p align="center" class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: center;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';">APPELLATE DIVISION</span></strong></p><p align="center" class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 6pt; text-align: center;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';">(CIVIL)</span></strong></p><table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="MsoNormalTable" style="margin-left: auto; border-collapse: collapse; margin-right: auto; padding: 0in 5.4pt; height: 112.85pt;"><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman'; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Government of Bangladesh and others </span></p><p align="right" class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 6.0pt; text-align: right; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';">. . . <u>Appellants</u></span></strong></p><p align="center" class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 6.0pt; text-align: center; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';">-Versus-</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman'; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">M/S. AMS Faraj Construction and another </span></p><p align="right" class="MsoNormal" style="text-align: right; mso-layout-grid-align: none; text-autospace: none;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';">. . .Respondents</span></strong></p></td></tr></tbody></table><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-top: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="color: #000000;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11pt; line-height: 110%;">Constitution of Bangladesh, 1972</span></strong></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"><span style="color: #000000;">Article 102</span></span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">The Agreement entered into by the writ-petitioner had no legal basis. Hence, there was no question of any right accruing in favour of the writ-petitioner. We say so, firstly because the Liquidator apparently did not exist at that time. Secondly, his power with regard to the Mill was only to the extent of selling the same through tender and not by any agreement. Every part of the transaction that followed, including payment and acceptance of installments was without lawful authority. In the facts of the instant case the contract entered into by the Official Liquidator is not a statutory contract, apart </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">from the fact that, the post of the liquidator had</span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> been abolished and in any event the </span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman Bold',serif; letter-spacing: -.3pt;">liquidator was authorised to sell the property</span></strong><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> in question through tender. <span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">         </span>. . . (24 and 25)</span></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; text-align: justify;"><strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">Sharping Matshajibi Samabaya Samity Ltd. Vs. Bangladesh and others 39 DLR (AD) 85; Bangladesh Power Development Board and others Vs. Md. Asaduzzaman Sikder, 9 BLC (AD)1 and Superintendent Engineer, RHD Sylhet and others Vs. Md. Eunus and Brothers (Pvt.) Ltd. 31 (AD)BLD 1 ref.</span></em></strong></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 1in; text-indent: -0.5in; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">For the Appellants :</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"> <span style="mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Mr. Mahbubey Alam, Attorney General, instructed by Mr. Haridas Paul, Advocate-on-Record </span></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 1in; text-indent: -0.5in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">For the Respondents </span></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">:</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;"> <span style="mso-spacerun: yes;"> </span><span style="mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Mr. Rokanuddin Mahmud, Senior <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">Advocate with Mr. Sheik Fazle</span>-Noor Taposh<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">  </span>and Mr. Mehedi Hasan, Advocates,<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span>instructed <span style="letter-spacing: -.4pt;">by Mrs. Madhumalati Chowdhury</span> Barua, Advocate-on-Record</span></span></p><p align="center" class="MsoNormal" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;">JUDGMENT</span></u></strong></p><p class="MsoBodyText2" style="margin-bottom: 12pt; text-indent: 27pt; line-height: normal; text-align: justify;"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><u><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-variant: small-caps;">Muhammad Imman Ali, J</span></u></strong><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; font-variant: small-caps;">:-</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt;"> This civil appeal, by leave, is directed against the judgement and order dated 15.06.2015 passed by a Division Bench of the High Court Division in Writ Petition No.10820 of 2014 disposing of the Rule <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">Nisi</em>. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">2.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">The facts of the case, in brief, are that the writ-petitioner (respondent No.1 herein) is a proprietorship firm established under the laws of Bangladesh which is represented by its proprietor, Md. Faruk Islam Bhuiyan. The Chisty Textile Mills Limited, Doulatpur, Cumilla (the Mill) was established as a private industry in 1962 which was incorporated under the Companies Act, 1913. After the independence of Bangladesh in 1971, along with other textile and jute mills of the country, the Mill was also nationalised and was taken over pursuant to President’s Order No.27 of 1972 and was vested upon Bangladesh Textile Mills Corporation (BTMC) for its maintenance, management and improvement. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">3.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Subsequently, as continuous loss was being incurred by the Mill, the Government closed down the operation of the Mill from the month of June in 1998. Thereafter, with the intention of privatising the Mill through the Privatisation Commission, the Government invited international tender. However, even after inviting international tender four times, the Mill was not sold for different reasons. Pursuant to the decision of the Bangladesh Government and by virtue of article 23(2) of the President’s Order No.27 of 1972 read with Order dated 01.11.2003 of the Ministry of Textiles and Jute, the Mill was declared to be wound up with effect from 09.11.2003. Accordingly, writ-respondent No.2 was appointed as the Liquidator for the Mill to take all actions required to be taken and all responsibilities required to be discharged in this regard vide Memo dated 13.11.2003. According to the letter dated 02.08.2003 signed by the Senior Assistant Secretary and the circular from the Cabinet Division, it was stated that all actions required to be taken and all responsibilities required to sell or discharge the Mill would also be monitored by the Ministry of Industry. The writ-petitioner vide different applications made to writ-respondent No.2 expressed its intention of purchasing the Mill. In response to the said applications, writ-respondent No.2 informed the writ-petitioner that a land dispute existed and only after disposal of the case regarding the land dispute and after review and assessing the valuation of all the movable and immovable properties, inventories and liabilities of the Mill by a CA firm, a tender should be floated to that effect. Subsequently, a CA firm was appointed in this regard which analysed and decided that the value of the assets of the Mill was taka 15.11 crore and liabilities were 44.56 crore. The writ-petitioner being aware of such valuation again expressed its intention to purchase the Mill. However, when the file was presented for approval before the Minister of the Ministry of Textiles and Jute, he declined to approve it and decided that the Mill would be sold at taka 35 crore in the meeting dated 04.07.2013, which was evident from the minutes of the meeting bearing reference dated 10.07.2013. The writ-petitioner was informed about the decision of the Minister vide letter dated 14.08.2013. Due to the subsisting recession at that time, the writ-petitioner was unable to pay the entire amount at once. Hence the writ-petitioner made a number of applications for paying the purchase price of taka 35 crore by making a down payment of taka 15 crore and paying the rest of taka 20 crore in instalments. The application was allowed and accordingly, the writ-petitioner vide pay order Nos.0000061 and 0847087 dated 13.11.2013 paid a total of taka 15 crore to writ-respondent No.2 which was duly received by him. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 105%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">4.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 105%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Thereafter, writ-respondent No.2 and the writ-petitioner entered into a deed of agreement for sale dated 01.01.2014 for sale of the movable and immovable assets of the non-operative Chisty Textile Mills Limited wherein it had been specifically agreed by the parties that upon payment by the writ-petitioner of the remaining balance of taka 20 crore in 5(five) equal instalments within 10 (ten) months from the date of the agreement, writ-respondent No.2 would have the legal obligation to execute and register necessary sale deed in favour of the writ-petitioner in order to transfer the ownership of Chisty Textile Mills Limited along with all its movable and immovable proprieties.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">5.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">In accordance with clause 3 of the agreement, the possession of the Mill was transferred and handed over to the writ-petitioner vide letter dated 06.01.2014 and subsequently, the writ-petitioner acknowledged the delivery of the possession of the Mills on 07.01.2014 vide Memo dated 09.01.2014.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">6.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">All of a sudden, writ-respondent No.2 issued a Memo dated 18.02.2014 restraining the writ-petitioner from carrying out any activity in relation to Chisty Textile Mills Limited until further instruction. In the said Memo, writ-respondent No.2 asserted that pursuant to some news report on 11.02.2014 in the Daily Janakantha and the Daily Sangbad which alleged some fraud with respect to the winding up of the Mill, all activities of the Mill should be stopped.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">7.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Upon receiving the said Memo dated 18.02.2014, the writ-petitioner made an application dated 24.02.2014 before respondent No.2 praying for cancellation of the same and reinstatement of the Memo dated 09.01.2014.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">8.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Thereafter, writ-respondent No.2, in accordance with the order of Prime Minister’s Office’s Memo dated 27.02.2014 issued Memo dated 17.04.2014 wherein, upon summarising the entire transaction it was stated that the operation of the said Mill has duly been stayed by writ-respondent No.2 and that the matter is under process and a meeting has been called in this regard. In the meantime, the writ-petitioner in accordance with the Agreement, paid the 1st instalment of taka 4.00 crore vide pay orders No.0850777 and 0850778 dated 13.04.2014 of taka 2.00 crore each along with fine of taka 5.00 lac by pay order No.0850776 dated 13.04.2014. Subsequently, the writ-petitioner expressed its willingness to pay the 2nd instalment of taka 4.00 crore to writ-respondent No.2, who verbally instructed the writ-petitioner, who accordingly made payment of the 2nd instalment vide pay order No.0006430 dated 30.006.2014 of taka 4.00 crore.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">9.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">             </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Thereafter, the writ-petitioner tried to deposit the 3rd instalment by pay order which writ-respondent No.2 was reluctant to receive. In this connection, the writ-petitioner vide letter dated 17.07.2014 prayed before respondent No.2 to accept the 3rd instalment along with the rest of the outstanding purchase price and thereafter execute a sale deed and register the same in favour of the writ-petitioner as agreed between the parties in the Agreement. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">10.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Having received no response from writ-respondent No.2, the writ-petitioner sent a letter to the Minister, Ministry of Jute and Textile dated 28.08.2014 concerning the same matter as stated above, but the writ-petitioner did not receive any reply.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">   </span></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">11.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Writ-respondent Nos.1 and 2 contested the writ-petition by filing affidavit-in-opposition contending, <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">inter alia,</em> that as per the terms and condition of clause 3 of the agreement for sale in question dated 01.01.2014, possession could be handed over after executing the final deed. Since question was raised in the process of transferring the mill in question, the authority issued the letter dated 18.02.2014 and thereby restrained the writ-petitioner from carrying out any activity in relation to the mill in question only in order to protect the property of the State.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">12.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">The further case of respondent No.1 is that serious allegation was raised for selling the Mill in question in such a way without holding any competent tender and when the matter came to the notice of the office of the Prime Minister, who of her own accord, formed an enquiry committee to enquire into the matter and who, after conducting enquiry on 31.08..2014, submitted a detailed report wherein it was categorically found that the entire selling process of the mill in question was done without floating public tender inasmuch as the same had been sold through the Liquidation Cell, which had been abolished on 16.02.2011.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">13.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Thereafter, the matter was sent to the Ministry of Law, Justice and Parliamentary Affairs for legal opinion who on 20.07.2014 opined that the entire sale process had been done illegally. After conducting the enquiry, by an office order dated 28.09.2014, the matter was intimated to the Liquidator, Liquidation Cell. Therefore, under such a compelling situation, writ-respondent No.1 was under a moral obligation to accept the defaulted instalment from the writ-petitioner.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">14.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">The further case of writ-respondent No.2 in short is that the writ-petitioner proved himself to be a defaulter and the writ-petitioner in making the payment of 2nd instalment caused much delay, inasmuch as according to the terms of the agreement the premium of 2nd instalment ought to have been paid by 30th April, 2014 and thereby the writ-petitioner himself violated the terms of the agreement dated 01.01.2014 and as such writ-respondent No.2 did not accept premium for 2nd instalment from the writ-petitioner.<span style="mso-spacerun: yes;">    </span></span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">15.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">By the impugned judgement and order, the High Court Division disposed of the Rule <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">Nisi </em>directing the writ-respondents to allow the writ-petitioner to resume its activities in relation to Chisty Textile Mills Limited and to receive the installments for the balance amount from the writ-petitioner and execute and register necessary sale deed in favour of the writ-petitioner in compliance with the terms of the Deed of Agreement for sale dated 01.01.2014 executed by writ-respondent No.2 in accordance with law. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">16.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Hence, the writ-respondents as the petitioners filed Civil Petition for Leave to Appeal No. 3468 of 2015. </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 24pt; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Leave was granted to consider the following grounds:</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 6pt 0.75in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">“I.</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;"><span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">  </span>Because, the subject matter of the writ-petition being a commercial contract and not the contract of the sovereign the same cannot be amenable in writ jurisdiction; the High Court Division committed error in entertaining the writ petition without considering the aforesaid aspect of the matter and hence the judgement and order of the High Court Division is liable to be set aside.</span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt 0.75in; text-indent: -0.25in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%;">II.</span></strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;"><span style="mso-tab-count: 1;">  </span>Because, the property in question namely ‘Chisty Textile Mills Ltd.’ is an important property of the Government and without holding any competent tender by floating public tender notice, selling of the property by negotiation through Liquidator is illegal, the High Court Division committed error in not considering the aforesaid aspect of the matter and thus the judgement and order of the High Court Division is liable to be set aside.” </span></p><p class="MsoNormal" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><strong><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Times New Roman';"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">17.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong><!--[endif]--><span style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; mso-bidi-font-weight: bold;">Mr. Mahbubey Alam, learned Attorney General, appearing on behalf of the appellants made submissions in line with the grounds upon which leave was granted. By reference to the decisions in the case of <strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">Sharping Matshajibi Samabaya Samity Ltd. Vs. Bangladesh &amp; ors. </em></strong>reported in <strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">39 DLR (AD)85</em></strong>, <strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">Bangladesh Power Development Board and others vs Md. Asaduzzaman Sikder</em></strong> reported in <strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">9 BLC (AD)1</em></strong> and <strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">Superintendent Engineer, RHD Sylhet and others v. Md. Eunus and Brothers (Pvt.) Ltd. </em></strong>reported in<strong><em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;"> 31 (AD)BLD 1,</em></strong> the learned Attorney General submitted that the contract in question was not a sovereign contract, but purely a commercial contract and as such the High Court Division erred in entertaining the matter under the writ jurisdiction. </span></p><p class="Style1" style="margin: 0in 0in 12pt; text-indent: 0in; line-height: 110%; text-align: justify;"><!-- [if !supportLists]--><span class="FontStyle11"><strong style="mso-bidi-font-weight: normal;"><span lang="EN-GB" style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman',serif;"><span style="mso-list: Ignore;">18.<span style="font: 7.0pt 'Times New Roman';">         </span></span></span></strong></span><!--[endif]--><span lang="EN-GB" style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman',serif;">Mr. Rokanuddin Mahmud, learned Counsel with Mr. Sheik Fazle-Noor Taposh, and Mr. Mehedi Hasan Chowdhury, learned Advocates, appearing on behalf of the respondents made submissions in support of the impugned judgement and order of the High Court Division. It was further submitted that</span><span class="FontStyle11"><span lang="EN-GB" style="font-size: 11.0pt; line-height: 110%; font-family: 'Times New Roman',serif; mso-bidi-font-family: 'Book Antiqua';"> the decision of the Bangladesh Government by virtue of Article 23 (2) of the President Order 27 of 1972 was that with order dated 01.11.2003 of the Ministry of Textiles and Jute, the Mill in question was declared to be wound up with effect from 09.11.2003. Accordingly, on 13.11.2003 liquidator was appointed who disposed of the Mill in favour of the writ-petitioner in a transparent way, and before that 2 times the matter was put in auction, but competent bidder was not found and continuous loss was being incurred from the Mill. Subsequently, the writ-petitioner purchased the Mill at the highest rate of Tk. 35 crore. It was further submitted that the contract dated 01.01.2014 was confirmed in between the parties and part consideration was paid and the writ petitioner did not breach any condition of contract for sale so, the seller has no option to go beyond the agreement for sale and the Ministry of Textile and Jute has legal obligation to perform its part of the contract. The price settled at Tk. 35 crore is proper value of the property in question and part consideration money was received without any objection so, the appellants subsequently cannot refuse to perform their part of the contract which is illegal. It was also submitted that the appellants have most arbitrarily, illegally and with <em style="mso-bidi-font-style: normal;">mala fide</em> intention restrained the respondents from carrying on any activity in relation to the Mill although the respondent <span style="letter-spacing: -.3pt;">has lawful possession in the same in accordance</span> with the agreement. It was submitted that the appellants restrained the respondents from carrying out any activity in relation to the Mill without giving opportunity to be heard and as such, the actions of the appellants have violated the principle of natural justice. The appellant's action in restraining the writ-petitioner from carrying on any activity related to the Mill and their deliberate failure to receive installment for the balance amount from the respondents and execute and register necessary sale deed in favour of them in compliance with the terms of the Agreement have clearly infringed the writ-petitioner's fundamental right to property guaranteed and protected under Article 42 of Part III of the Constitution of the People's Republic of Bangladesh and as such, direction upon the appellants to allow the respondents to resume all activities in relation to the Mill and also to receive the installment for the balance amount from the writ-petitioner-respondent and to execute and register necessary sale deed in order to transfer the ownership of the Mill along with all its movable and immovable properties in compliance with the terms of the agreement is justified. The respondents have the fundamental right to indiscriminately enjoy the protection of law and to be treated in accordance with law, and therefore the <span style="mso-bidi-font-style: italic;">action of the appellant in restraining the respondent</span> from carrying on any activity related to the Mill is clearly detrimental.
